'From Bowling Green, to Speakership, to the White House'

Even with a plurality, most expected you would win the day. Indeed, you have won the day. A large mandate, an increased House majority, and possibly the United States Senate. This may be the first trifecta for Democrats in nearly twenty years. You're the first person since James Polk to rise to the Presidency from the Speakership, and no former slave state has had a President since the discredited Andrew Johnson.

Oscar Underwood is due to be the next Speaker, unless you wish to support someone else's bid. In any case, the next Speaker of the House should be a strong ally as you attempt to pass a variety of progressive bills into law. Republicans are likely to reunify between now and 1916, with the nominee being anyone's guess - but if you act quickly, who is to say you can't gain some of Roosevelt's supporters with legislative overtures? Best of luck to you!

Gridlock and Deadlock

This was always a possibility, but a slim one. Nevertheless, no candidate shall have an electoral college majority. 'I Think We've Got Another Washington... And Wilson Is His Name'

Within a few hours, it becomes clear that this is no ordinary victory. No, this is not even the victory you had hoped for. It is nothing short of a miracle - no one could envision any man receiving such a large mandate by the American people. On This Day in History: Former President Thomas Marshall Vanishes Climbing Mount Everest, Smithsonian Magazine | May 17, 2023

Thomas Marshall wore many a hat during his Presidency. Among the feathers in the first Hoosier president's cap are a staunch opposition to eugenics, progressive reform with repsect for the states, and a cautious foreign policy, however controversial in the minds of historians today some of the latter have become. Even so, Marshall is remembered in the public imagination not for being one of only two Democrats to occupy the White House in the fourth party system, and not for his self-depracating humor either, but for the circumstances following his later disappearance on the treacherous slopes of Mount Everest one hundred years ago today.

Marshall's narrow defeat in 1916 to Leonard Wood reportedly left him bitter and frustrated, and he almost entirely secluded himself from the public for the remainder of the decade. However, as the economy took off in the 1920s, the former President soon found an interest in exploration. Having seen all there is to see in the realm of domestic politics, he turned to conquering the corners of nature, helping finance a 1920 expedition to the South Pole. By 1922, Marshall's increasing interest in mountaineering had led him to meet English explorer George Mallory, and the two arranged an ambitious expedition to summit Mount Everest in May 1923.

At first, the expedition got off to a rocky start, taking unusually long to ascend the mountain's North Ridge after weather delays and equipment malfunctions. However, the team rallied after the weather subsided, establishing a well-supplied camp at about 27,000 feet above sea level. It was at this point that Marshall joined Mallory's advance team with his own suite of sherpas.

On May 17, Mallory, followed by climbers Andrew Irvine and Elliot B. Moseley was about to dispatch for the final climb to the top when they were stopped by an animated Marshall. The former president, clad in a climbing suit and oxygen tanks, expressed his desire to witness "the splendor of God's kingdom from its highest point." Mallory agreed, and the four climbers departed for the summit at 6:17 AM. They were never seen again, although evidence was later found suggesting that they passed the Second Step and neared the summit, marking their final location as much closer to the summit than previously realized.

Over the next three months, a search party scoured the slopes of Everest, but no trace of the climbers, or the former president, were ever found. It would be another 24 years until Sir Albert Pendelton summitted Everest in 1947, laying at the summit a plaque dedicated to Mallory, Irvine, Moseley, and Marshall.

Some have suspected that Marshall was murdered by the Mafia, citing his reformist policies and debts to them over mountaineering as a potential motive to assassinate the former president. Others believe that Marshall faked his death to escape these debts, descending the slope on an alternate route and using local sherpas to reach civilization under an assumed identity. However, the most likely explanation is perhaps the most straightforward one: that, like thousands of explorers before him, he gallantly gave his life in pursuit of human knowledge and achievement.

Since late 1915, the United States had been militarily involved in the conflict after Roosevelt requested Congress give him a declaration of war following U-boat attacks by the Germans.

After a tough loss in 1912, the Democrats nominated anti-war business magnate Henry Ford, who promised to resolve the conflict and restore peace in Europe. Meanwhile, President Roosevelt ran as both a member of his Progressive Party, and attempted to secure the Republican Party nomination as well. However, Robert La Follette was able to do so after positioning himself as the anti-Roosevelt candidate, and was nominated by the remains of a party that had continuously lost seats since 1910. The campaign was a long one, and mostly centered around the candidates debating the logistics and causes of the war. Ford and La Follette clearly split the anti-war vote, and Roosevelt aggressively campaigned in the South as a way to avoid a potential deadlock.

And here are the results...

President Roosevelt won re-election, becoming the first third-party to win re-election. Roosevelt recieved 273 electoral votes, Ford won 250 electoral votes, and poor La Follette only got 8 electoral votes.

67.41% of the population voted in this election.
